Conflicting Evidence in Mahr Discharges
Ruling on conflicting evidence in mahr cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for precedence of evidence when the husband and heirs dispute over the timing of a mahr waiver.

Core Idea
When husband and heirs present conflicting evidence on whether waiver occurred in health or death-illness, the evidence of health (husband's) is given precedence.

Disputes often arise after the wife's death: was the waiver during health (valid) or death-illness (invalid)? The Hanafi school has a clear rule for weighing evidence, prioritizing the proof of health. We examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Husband claims the wife discharged him during health; heirs claim she discharged during death-illness.
2
State the Hanafi rule
The evidence of health (husband's evidence) is given precedence over the heirs' evidence of death-illness.
This is the preponderant (al-rājiḥ) view.
3
Explain the rationale
The husband is confirming a discharge, which is a release from debt; the default is that one is free from debt. The heirs claim against the default. Also, the husband's evidence involves an earlier event (health) which is more likely to be truthful as it is against his interest? Actually, the rationale is that the health evidence is more consistent with the principle that the wife owned the mahr and could dispose of it freely during health.
The text does not give an explicit rationale but contrasts with the view that heirs' evidence is first.
4
Note the alternative view
Some Hanafi scholars said the heirs' evidence is first because they are protecting inheritance rights.
But the preponderant view is the first.

And in Kitab and al-Tajnis: She gifted the mahr to her young son and the father accepted – the chosen view is that it is not valid because it is a gift not taken into possession. End quote. And in it: She said to her husband: 'If the mahr matters to you, I have discharged you' – he is discharged immediately and it is not a suspension. And if he divorced his wife thrice and she did not know, then he said to her: 'If you do not discharge me from the mahr, you are thrice divorced' – she discharged him and he accepted – he is discharged. Abu Hamid said: He is discharged whether he accepted or not. And if she said: 'The dower that I have on my husband is the property of so-and-so, I have no right in it' and the assignee confirms her, then she discharges her husband from it, he is discharged. And if she said: 'The mahr that I have on my husband is for my father' – her acknowledgment is not valid. End quote. From al-Nikah: They differed regarding the gift of mahr; she said: 'I gifted it to you on condition that you do not divorce me' and he said: 'without condition' – the word is hers. End quote. And it is mentioned in al-Da'wa: If both bring evidence, the woman's evidence is given precedence; some said the husband's evidence is precedence. And for the validity of her waiver, she must not be terminally ill, as is known in the waiver of the heir. And in al-Khulasa on mahr: She gifted her mahr to her husband and then she died, then her heirs and the husband differed: the heirs said the gift was during death-illness, the husband said it was during health – the word is that of the husband because he denies the mahr. End quote. And in al-Qunya from Kitab al-Hiba: She gifted her mahr to her husband during her death-illness and her husband died before her – she has no claim because the discharge is valid as long as she does not die; if she died after him, her heirs have the claim for her mahr. End quote. And in it also from the chapter of contradictory evidence: The husband brought evidence that she discharged him from the dower during her health, and the heirs brought evidence that she discharged him during her death-illness – the evidence of health is given precedence; some said the heir's evidence is precedence. End quote. And the preponderant is the first. And in it also from al-Hiba: He discharged him from a debt to fix his affair with the sultan – he is not discharged, and it is bribery. And if he refused to lie with his wife and said to her: 'Discharge me from the mahr so that I may lie with you' – she discharged him; it is said he is discharged because the discharge is for affection that calls for intercourse, and the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Give gifts to one another, you will love one another' – unlike the first discharge because it is solely for fixing a matter, and fixing a matter is a religious duty on him, and spending wealth on what is a duty is bribery. End quote. And in it from Kitab al-Da'wa: A woman died; her husband asked her heirs for his discharge from the mahr, they refused, so he paid the mahr; then he found evidence that his wife had discharged him during her health and he did not know – he may take back what he gave (the mahr) as a matter of religion; this suggests he cannot claim it back judicially. End quote. And in it from the chapter of contradictory evidence: The woman brought evidence for the mahr that her husband had acknowledged it until today, and the husband brought evidence that she had discharged him from this mahr she claims – the evidence of discharge is given precedence; similarly with debt. End quote. And it is required for the validity of her discharge from the mahr that she knows its meaning, because in al-Tajnis: If he said to her 'Say: I have gifted my mahr to you' and she said that while not knowing Arabic, it is not valid. There is a distinction between this and manumission and divorce – they take effect because for them, knowledge is not a condition; the difference is that consent is a condition for the validity of gift (hiba) but not for manumission and divorce. End quote. His statement: 'And in it she said to her husband' – meaning in al-Qunya from Kitab al-Mudayanat also. And he indicated.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This lengthy passage from page 265 presents numerous specific rulings on waiver of mahr. It begins with a case from al-Tajnis: a woman gifting her mahr to her young son – it is invalid because the son (a minor) cannot take possession through his father's acceptance; the gift is not effectively delivered (ghayr maqbuda). Next, a woman says 'if the mahr matters to you, I have discharged you' – this is not a conditional suspension; it is an immediate discharge. Then a scenario where a husband threatens triple divorce if she does not discharge him; she does so and he accepts – he is discharged. Abu Hamid adds that he is discharged whether he accepts or not. Then cases of conflicting acknowledgments: if she says her mahr belongs to someone else and that person confirms, then she discharges her husband, it is valid; but if she says it belongs to her father, her acknowledgment is not valid (because it is a claim against herself without benefit). Then a dispute (ikhtilaf) over the gift of mahr: she claims it was conditional on no divorce, he says unconditional – her word is taken. Then the rule that for a valid waiver, she must not be terminally ill (marid marad al-mawt) because that would affect the rights of her heirs. If she dies and there is a dispute between heirs and husband over whether the gift was during health or death-illness, the husband's word is taken (as he denies owing the mahr). Similarly, if both bring evidence, the husband's evidence is given precedence if it shows discharge during health. But there is a difference of opinion; the preponderant (al-rajih) is the first (health evidence preferred). Also, a discharge given to effect a bribe (to fix a matter with the sultan) is invalid because it is bribery. In contrast, a discharge given so that the husband will lie with her is valid, because it is for mutual affection and supported by the hadith 'give gifts to one another'. Finally, it is a condition that the woman understands the meaning of what she says; if she does not know Arabic and utters the words without understanding, the discharge is invalid, unlike divorce and manumission which do not require understanding because they are effective by utterance alone. The passage ends with a marginal note referring back to earlier content.

Key Terms
بينة الصحة (bayyinat al-ṣiḥḥa): Evidence of health; proof that the waiver occurred while the wife was healthy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When husband and heirs dispute over the timing of a mahr waiver, which evidence is given precedence according to the preponderant Hanafi view?
A) The heirs' evidence of death-illness
B) The husband's evidence of health
C) Both are rejected
D) The court asks the wife's mother

Key Takeaway
In conflicting evidence over timing of mahr waiver, the evidence of health is given precedence over evidence of death-illness.

Conditions for Valid Remission of Mahr
Compilation of rulings on wife's remission of mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for a wife's remission of mahr to be valid and apply them to the scenarios listed in the passage.

Core Idea
A wife's remission of mahr requires her consent, knowledge of its meaning, and absence of terminal illness or bribery.

This lesson extracts from the compilation in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq the key conditions that render a wife's remission (ibra') of her dower legally effective. The passage presents various scenarios—gifting to a minor son, remission under threat, remission for a bribe—and each clarifies a specific condition. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the rulings on mahr in marital disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
Recognize that the wife's remission of mahr is a form of gift (hiba). It requires a valid offer and acceptance.
Ibra' is treated as hiba in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Extract conditions from scenarios
Go through each scenario in the passage and infer the condition it demonstrates: e.g., the requirement of the wife's knowledge (الرضا شرط جواز الهبة) from the Arabic language example.
Focus on the scenario of the wife not understanding Arabic.
3
Synthesize the list
Compile all conditions: (1) wife must understand the meaning of ibra'; (2) not during terminal illness; (3) not a bribe; (4) valid consent (not coerced).
Also note that acceptance by husband may not be required (Abu Hamid's view).
4
Apply to new scenario
Test your list against a hypothetical: e.g., a wife in perfect health says 'I forgive the mahr' in Arabic but under duress—determine validity.
Duress invalidates consent, so remission is void.

Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. In the book [al-Tajnis]: She gifted the mahr to her minor son and the father accepted; the chosen view is that it is not valid because it is a gift not taken possession of. End. In it: She said to her husband, 'If the mahr concerns you, I have relieved you of it'; he is immediately relieved, not suspended. And if he divorced his wife three times without her knowledge, then said to her, 'If you do not relieve me of the mahr, you are thrice divorced,' and she relieved him and he accepted, he is relieved. Abu Hamid said: he is relieved whether he accepted or not. And if she said, 'The dowry that I have against my husband belongs to so-and-so, I have no right to it,' and the beneficiary confirmed her, then she relieved her husband of it, he is relieved. And if she said, 'The mahr that I have against my husband belongs to my father,' her acknowledgment is not valid. End. (From) the book of Nikah from it: They disputed regarding the gift of mahr; she said, 'I gifted it to you on condition that you do not divorce me,' and he said, 'Without condition'; the say-so is hers. End. And it is mentioned in al-Da'wa: If both produce evidence, the woman's evidence is given precedence, and it is said the husband's evidence is given precedence. For the validity of her remission, it is necessary that she not be suffering from a terminal illness, as known regarding the remission of an heir. In al-Khulasah regarding mahr: She gifted her mahr to her husband and died, then her heirs and the husband disputed: the heirs said the gift was during terminal illness, the husband said during health; the say-so is the husband's because he denies the mahr. End. In al-Qunyah from the book of Gift: She gifted her mahr to her husband during her terminal illness and her husband died before her; there is no claim for her for the validity of the remission as long as she did not die; when she dies from it, her heirs have a claim for her mahr. End. Also in it from the chapter of two conflicting evidences: The husband produced evidence that she relieved him of the dowry during her health, and the heirs produced evidence that she relieved him during her terminal illness; the evidence of health is given precedence, and it is said the evidence of the heir is given precedence. End. The first is preponderant. Also in it from Gift: He relieved him of a debt to fix his affair before the sultan; he is not relieved and it is a bribe. And if he refused to lie with his wife, and she said, 'Relieve me of the mahr and I will lie with you,' and she relieved him, it is said he is relieved because the remission is for affection leading to intercourse; and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other.' Unlike the remission in the first case because it was confined to fixing the affair, and fixing the affair is owed religiously, and spending money for what is owed is the limit of bribery. End. In it from the book of Da'wa: A woman died; her husband asked her heirs for his discharge from the mahr; they refused, so he paid the mahr; then he found evidence that his wife had relieved him during health and he did not know; so he may take back what he gave regarding the mahr religiously; this indicates that he cannot reclaim it judicially. End. In it from the chapter of two conflicting evidences: The woman produced evidence of the mahr that her husband had acknowledged it until this day, and the husband produced evidence that she had relieved him of this mahr; the evidence of remission is given precedence. And similarly for a debt. End. The validity of her remission of the mahr requires her knowledge of its meaning. As in al-Tajnis: If he said to her, 'Say: I have gifted my mahr to you,' and she said it while not knowing Arabic, it is not valid; a distinction is made between this and manumission and divorce, where they do occur. The difference is that consent is a condition for the permissibility of gift, not a condition for the permissibility of manumission and divorce. End. His saying: (And in it she said to her husband) i.e., in al-Qunyah from the book of Loans also. And the author indicated that if he married her for a hundred dinars on condition that she remit fifty and she accepted, it is valid a fortiori, as in al-Khaniyyah.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from various Hanafi sources (al-Tajnis, al-Khulasah, al-Qunyah, etc.) regarding the wife's remission (ibra') of her mahr. The author aggregates multiple scenarios to delineate the conditions for a valid remission. Key points: (1) A wife cannot gift the mahr to her minor son through the father's acceptance because it is a gift not physically transferred. (2) Remission can be immediate or conditional, but if a husband threatens divorce, remission is valid upon acceptance. (3) If a wife acknowledges that the mahr belongs to another, it is valid with the beneficiary's confirmation, but not if she claims it belongs to her father. (4) In disputes about whether a remission occurred during health or terminal illness, the husband's denial is given weight, and evidence of health is preferred over evidence of deathbed remission. (5) Remission given in exchange for the husband's sexual intimacy is valid because it fosters love, as per the hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other.' (6) However, remission given to bribe an official is invalid. (7) The wife must understand the meaning of 'remission' for it to be valid; if she says the words without knowing Arabic, it is invalid, unlike divorce and manumission which do not require her understanding. (8) A conditional remission of part of the mahr is valid. The passage underscores that the wife's consent and knowledge are crucial for ibra', distinguishing it from other legal acts.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): The wife's remission or discharge of the mahr (dower) owed by the husband, effectively waiving her right to it.
الصداق (al-sadaq): The mahr or dower given by the husband to the wife as an essential part of the marriage contract.
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition is highlighted by the scenario of a wife saying 'I have gifted my mahr' in a language she does not understand?
A) The husband's acceptance
B) The wife's knowledge of the meaning
C) Not being terminally ill
D) Not being a bribe

Key Takeaway
A wife's remission of mahr is valid only if she understands its meaning and is not coerced, bribed, or terminally ill.

Remission of Mahr for Intimacy and Gift-Giving
Comparison of valid and invalid purposes for remission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why remission of mahr for the purpose of intimacy is valid while remission for fixing a worldly affair is invalid, and apply the hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other' to legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Remission of mahr for affection and intimacy is valid as a gift, but remission to bribe an official is invalid.

This lesson focuses on two contrasting scenarios from the passage: remission given in exchange for the husband's sexual intimacy is valid, while remission given to help the husband fix an affair with the sultan is invalid (bribery). The key distinction lies in the intent—affection vs. worldly gain—supported by the hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Locate the scenario where the wife remits the mahr for intimacy and the one where the husband remits a debt to fix an affair.
The first is from the wife's perspective, the second from the husband's.
2
Analyze the intent
For the intimacy scenario, the intent is to foster love (التودد الداعي في الجماع). For the bribe scenario, the intent is to accomplish a worldly matter (إصلاح المهم).
The passage contrasts these directly.
3
Apply the hadith
The hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other' supports the first scenario as a legitimate gift. The second scenario is excluded because it is not a gift for love but a bribe.
This is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) used for derivation.
4
Derive the ruling
Remission for affection is valid; remission for bribery is invalid and remains a debt.
The ruling is based on the intent and the nature of the transaction.

كتاب النكاح باب المهر وفي كتاب وفي التجنيس : وهبت المهر لابنها الصغير وقبل الأب فالمختار أنها لا تصح لأنها هبة غير مقبوضة ا.هـ. وفيها قالت لزوجها إن كان يهمك المهر فقد أبرأتك يبرأ في الحال وليس بتعليق، ولو طلق امرأته ثلاثاً ولم تعلم به ثم قال لها إن لم تبرئيني من المهر فأنت طالق ثلاثاً فأبرأته وقبل يبرأ . وقال أبو حامد يبرأ قبل أو لم يقبل. ولو قالت الصداق الذي لي على زوجي ملك فلان بن فلان لا حق لي فيه وصدقها المقر له ثم أبرأت زوجها عنه يبرأ ولو قالت المهر الذي لي على زوجي لوالدي لا يصح إقرارها به ا هـ . النكاح منها : اختلفا في هبة المهر فقالت وهبته لك بشرط أن لا تطلقني وقال بغير شرط فالقول قولها اهـ. وذكر في الدعوى: لو أقاما البينة فبينة المرأة أولى، وقيل بينة الزوج أولى، ولا بد في صحة حطها من أن لا تكون مريضة مرض الموت لما عرف في إبراء الوارث. وفي الخلاصة من المهر وهبت مهرها من الزوج وماتت ثم اختلفت ورثتها مع الزوج قالت الورثة كانت الهبة في مرض الموت وقال الزوج كانت في الصحة فالقول قول الزوج لأنه ينكر المهر ا هـ. وفي القنية من كتاب الهبة وهبت مهرها من زوجها في مرض موتها ومات زوجها قبلها فلا دعوى لها لصحة الإبراء ما لم تمت فإذا ماتت منه فلورثتها دعوى مهرها ا.هـ. وفيها أيضاً من باب البينتين المتضادتين : أقام الزوج بينة أنها أبرأته من الصداق حال صحتها وأقام الورثة بينة أنها أبرأته في مرض موتها فبينة الصحة أولى، وقيل بينة الوارث أولى اهـ. والراجح الأول وفيها أيضاً من الهبة أبرأه عن الدين ليصلح مهمه عند السلطان لا يبرأ وهو رشوة، ولو أبى الاضطجاع عند امرأته فقال لها أبرئيني من المهر فأضطجع معك فأبرأته قيل يبرأ لأن الإبراء للتودد الداعي في الجماع وقال عليه السلام تهادوا تحابوا بخلاف الإبراء في الأول لأنه مقصور على إصلاح المهم وإصلاح المهم مستحق عليه ديانة وبذل المال فيما هو مستحق عليه حد الرشوة ا هـ. وفيها من كتاب الدعوى امرأة ماتت فطلب زوجها من ورثتها براءته من المهر فأبوا فأعطى المهر ثم ظهر له بينة أن امرأته أبرأته في حال الصحة ولم يعلم الزوج بذلك فله أن بما أعطى من يرجع المهر ديانة فهذا يشير إلى أنه لا يرجع عليهم قضاء ا.هـ. وفيها من باب البينتين المتضادتين : أقامت المرأة بينة على المهر على أن زوجها كان مقراً بذلك إلى يومنا هذا وأقام الزوج البينة أنها أبرأته من هذا المهر الذي تدعي فبينة البراءة أولى، وكذا في الدين ا هـ . ويشترط في صحة إبرائها عن المهر علمها بمعناها لما في التجنيس : لو قال لها قولي وهبت مهري منك فقالت المرأة ذلك وهي لا تحسن العربية لا يصح، فرق بين وبين العتق والطلاق حيث يقعان والفرق أن الرضا شرط جواز الهبة وليس بشرط لجواز العتق والطلاق ا هـ . قوله : ( وفيها قالت لزوجها أي في القنية من كتاب المداينات أيضاً. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو تزوجها بمائة دينار على أن تحط عنه خمسين منها فقبلت فهو صحيح بالأولى كما في الخانية .
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. In the book [al-Tajnis]: She gifted the mahr to her minor son and the father accepted; the chosen view is that it is not valid because it is a gift not taken possession of. End. In it: She said to her husband, 'If the mahr concerns you, I have relieved you of it'; he is immediately relieved, not suspended. And if he divorced his wife three times without her knowledge, then said to her, 'If you do not relieve me of the mahr, you are thrice divorced,' and she relieved him and he accepted, he is relieved. Abu Hamid said: he is relieved whether he accepted or not. And if she said, 'The dowry that I have against my husband belongs to so-and-so, I have no right to it,' and the beneficiary confirmed her, then she relieved her husband of it, he is relieved. And if she said, 'The mahr that I have against my husband belongs to my father,' her acknowledgment is not valid. End. (From) the book of Nikah from it: They disputed regarding the gift of mahr; she said, 'I gifted it to you on condition that you do not divorce me,' and he said, 'Without condition'; the say-so is hers. End. And it is mentioned in al-Da'wa: If both produce evidence, the woman's evidence is given precedence, and it is said the husband's evidence is given precedence. For the validity of her remission, it is necessary that she not be suffering from a terminal illness, as known regarding the remission of an heir. In al-Khulasah regarding mahr: She gifted her mahr to her husband and died, then her heirs and the husband disputed: the heirs said the gift was during terminal illness, the husband said during health; the say-so is the husband's because he denies the mahr. End. In al-Qunyah from the book of Gift: She gifted her mahr to her husband during her terminal illness and her husband died before her; there is no claim for her for the validity of the remission as long as she did not die; when she dies from it, her heirs have a claim for her mahr. End. Also in it from the chapter of two conflicting evidences: The husband produced evidence that she relieved him of the dowry during her health, and the heirs produced evidence that she relieved him during her terminal illness; the evidence of health is given precedence, and it is said the evidence of the heir is given precedence. End. The first is preponderant. Also in it from Gift: He relieved him of a debt to fix his affair before the sultan; he is not relieved and it is a bribe. And if he refused to lie with his wife, and she said, 'Relieve me of the mahr and I will lie with you,' and she relieved him, it is said he is relieved because the remission is for affection leading to intercourse; and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other.' Unlike the remission in the first case because it was confined to fixing the affair, and fixing the affair is owed religiously, and spending money for what is owed is the limit of bribery. End. In it from the book of Da'wa: A woman died; her husband asked her heirs for his discharge from the mahr; they refused, so he paid the mahr; then he found evidence that his wife had relieved him during health and he did not know; so he may take back what he gave regarding the mahr religiously; this indicates that he cannot reclaim it judicially. End. In it from the chapter of two conflicting evidences: The woman produced evidence of the mahr that her husband had acknowledged it until this day, and the husband produced evidence that she had relieved him of this mahr; the evidence of remission is given precedence. And similarly for a debt. End. The validity of her remission of the mahr requires her knowledge of its meaning. As in al-Tajnis: If he said to her, 'Say: I have gifted my mahr to you,' and she said it while not knowing Arabic, it is not valid; a distinction is made between this and manumission and divorce, where they do occur. The difference is that consent is a condition for the permissibility of gift, not a condition for the permissibility of manumission and divorce. End. His saying: (And in it she said to her husband) i.e., in al-Qunyah from the book of Loans also. And the author indicated that if he married her for a hundred dinars on condition that she remit fifty and she accepted, it is valid a fortiori, as in al-Khaniyyah.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from various Hanafi sources (al-Tajnis, al-Khulasah, al-Qunyah, etc.) regarding the wife's remission (ibra') of her mahr. The author aggregates multiple scenarios to delineate the conditions for a valid remission. Key points: (1) A wife cannot gift the mahr to her minor son through the father's acceptance because it is a gift not physically transferred. (2) Remission can be immediate or conditional, but if a husband threatens divorce, remission is valid upon acceptance. (3) If a wife acknowledges that the mahr belongs to another, it is valid with the beneficiary's confirmation, but not if she claims it belongs to her father. (4) In disputes about whether a remission occurred during health or terminal illness, the husband's denial is given weight, and evidence of health is preferred over evidence of deathbed remission. (5) Remission given in exchange for the husband's sexual intimacy is valid because it fosters love, as per the hadith 'Exchange gifts, you will love each other.' (6) However, remission given to bribe an official is invalid. (7) The wife must understand the meaning of 'remission' for it to be valid; if she says the words without knowing Arabic, it is invalid, unlike divorce and manumission which do not require her understanding. (8) A conditional remission of part of the mahr is valid. The passage underscores that the wife's consent and knowledge are crucial for ibra', distinguishing it from other legal acts.

Key Terms
الرشوة (al-rishwa): Bribery: giving property to achieve a worldly goal that is religiously obligatory or to circumvent justice.
التودد (al-tawaddud): Affection, friendliness; the intended effect of a valid gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason that remission of mahr given in exchange for sexual intimacy is valid?
A) Because it is a form of bribery
B) Because it is a gift meant to foster love, supported by hadith
C) Because the husband accepted
D) Because the wife was healthy

Key Takeaway
Remission of mahr given to encourage love and intimacy is valid; remission given to bribe or achieve a worldly goal is invalid.

Disputes Over Remission: Health vs. Terminal Illness
Conflict of evidence in remission disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that evidence of health is preponderant over evidence of terminal illness in disputes over remission of mahr, and explain the practical consequence.

Core Idea
When conflicting evidence arises, the evidence of health prevails, discharging the husband.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in inheritance disputes: the wife remitted the mahr, but after her death, her heirs claim she was terminally ill, while the husband claims she was healthy. The passage presents a conflict of evidence (bayanat mutadaddah) and settles on the preponderance of the evidence of health. Understanding this tarjih is crucial for resolving such disputes in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize the conflict: wife's heirs claim remission occurred during terminal illness (so it is restricted to one-third), husband claims during health (so it is fully valid).
Both parties have evidence (bayanat).
2
Apply the tarjih rule
The passage states that the evidence of health is given precedence (بينة الصحة أولى). Some said evidence of the heir is first, but the preponderant view is the first.
The text explicitly says 'والراجح الأول'.
3
Understand the rationale
The Hanafi response (implied) relies on the principle of istishab (presumption of continuity of health) and that the husband's denial of the mahr is stronger because he denies a debt.
The passage also mentions that the husband's say-so is taken because he denies the mahr (ينكر المهر).
4
Apply the consequence
If health is proven, the husband is fully discharged and the heirs cannot claim the mahr. If illness is proven, the remission is treated as a bequest and is valid only up to one-third of the estate.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Valid Khulwah Equals Intercourse for Full Mahr
Ruling with proofs: khulwah equals intercourse for mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a valid khulwah obligates the full mahr, identify the proofs (ijma and hadith), and explain the qiyas to sale.

Core Idea
A valid khulwah after marriage obligates full mahr like actual intercourse, based on consensus and hadith.

This lesson examines the core Hanafi principle that a valid seclusion (khulwah) between spouses after a valid marriage contract obligates the full mahr, even without actual intercourse. We will explore the textual proofs and analogical reasoning that support this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Locate the author's statement that valid khulwah is like intercourse. Note the ruling: full mahr becomes wajib.
2
Examine the proofs
Identify the proofs cited: ijma of the Companions (reported by al-Tahawi), the hadith of al-Daraqutni, and the qiyas to sale. Understand how each proof supports the ruling.
The hadith: 'Whoever uncovers a woman's head covering or looks at her, the dowry is obligatory.'
3
Understand the interpretation of the Quranic verse
Understand how the word 'touch' (مس) in the verse is interpreted as seclusion (khulwah) by way of applying the name of the effect to the cause.
4
Analyze the branch case (furu)
Consider the example given: if a man commits adultery then marries the woman, he owes two mahr. This illustrates the strength of the khulwah principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والخلوة بلا مرض أحدهما وحيض ونفاس وإحرام وصوم فرض كالوطء) بيان للسبب الثالث المكمل للمهر وهي الخلوة الصحيحة لأنها سلمت المبدل حيث رفعت الموانع وذلك وسعها فيتأكد حقها في البدل اعتباراً بالبيع، وقد حكى الطحاوي إجماع الصحابة عليه، ويدل عليه حديث الدارقطني من كشف خمار امرأة أو نظر إليها وجب الصداق، دخل أو لم يدخل وحينئذ فالمراد بالمس في قوله تعالى وإن طلقتموهن من قبل أن تمسوهن [البقرة: ٢٣٧] الخلوة إطلاقاً لاسم المسبب على السبب إذا لمس مسبب عن الخلوة عادة ويكون كما له بالجماع بحضرة الناس بالإجماع لا بالآية ومن فروع لزوم المهر بالخلوة لو زني بامرأة فتزوجها وهو على بطنها فعليه مهران مهر بالزنا لأنه سقط الحد بالتزوج قبل تمام الزنا، والمهر المسمى بالنكاح لأن هذا يزيد على الخلوة. وقد شرط المصنف في إقامتها مقام الوطء شروطاً ترجع إلى أربعة أشياء : الخلوة الحقيقية، وعدم مانع حسي، وعدم مانع طبعي، وعدم مانع شرعي من الوطء فالأول للاحتراز عما إذا كان هناك ثالث فليست بخلوة، سواء كان ذلك الثالث بصيراً أو أعمى أو يقظاناً أو نائماً بالغاً أو صبياً يعقل. وفصل في المبتغى في الأعمى فإن لم يقف على حاله تصح وإن كان أصم إن كان نهاراً لا تصح وإن كان ليلاً تع تصح وشمل الثالث زوجته الأخرى وهو المذهب بناء على كراهة وطئها بحضرة ضرتها. واختلف في الجارية على أقوال ؛ قيل لا تمنع مطلقاً ولو كانت جارية لغيرهما، وقيل جاريتها تمنع بخلاف جاريته والمختار أن جاريتها لا تمنع كجاريته كما في الخلاصة وعليه الفتوى كما في المبتغى، وجزم الإمام السرخسي في المبسوط بأن كلاً منهما يمنع وهو قول أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه لأنه يمتنع من غشيانها بين يدي أمته طبعاً ا هـ. وشمل الثالث الكلب إن كان عقوراً مطلقاً وإن لم يكن عقوراً فكذلك إن كان لها، وإن كان له صحت الخلوة. وخرج الثالث الصبي الذي لا يعقل والمجنون والمغمى عليه والمراد بالذي يعقل هنا ما يمكنه أن يعبر ما يكون بينهما كما في الخانية وللاحتراز عن مكان لا يصلح للخلوة، والصالح لها أن يأمنا فيه اطلاع غيرهما عليهما كالدار والبيت ولو لم يكن له سقف، وكذا الخيمة في المفازة والمحل الذي عليه قبة مضروبة وكذا البستان الذي له باب وأغلق فلا تصح في المسجد والطريق الأعظم والحمام وسطح الدار من غير ساتر والبستان الذي ليس له باب وإن لم يكن هناك أحد، واختلف في البيت إذا كان بابه مفتوحاً أو طوابقه بحيث لو نظر إنسان رآهما ففي من هـ. قوله : (وشمل الثالث) أي الواقع في قوله للاحتراز عما إذا كان هناك ثالث قوله : ( وللاحتراز عن مكان لا يصلح للخلوة) عطف على قوله للاحتراز عما إذا كان هناك ثالث قوله : (لأن مرضه لا
His saying: (And seclusion without illness of either spouse, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, ihram, and obligatory fasting, is like intercourse). Explanation of the third cause completing the mahr, which is valid seclusion, because she has surrendered the exchanged [herself] by removing impediments and that is within her capacity, so her right to the exchange is confirmed by analogy to sale. Al-Tahawi narrated consensus of the Companions upon it. And it is indicated by the hadith of al-Daraqutni: 'Whoever uncovers a woman's head covering or looks at her, the dowry is obligatory, whether he entered or not.' Consequently, the intended meaning of 'touch' in His saying 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them' [al-Baqarah:237] is seclusion, applying the name of the effect to the cause, since touch is usually an effect of seclusion. And it is like intercourse in the presence of people by consensus, not by the verse. Among the branches of the obligation of mahr by seclusion: If a man committed adultery with a woman, then married her while he was on her belly, he incurs two mahr: the mahr for adultery because the hadd punishment is dropped by marriage before completion of the adultery, and the named mahr for marriage because this exceeds seclusion. The author stipulated for it taking the place of intercourse four categories of conditions: actual seclusion, absence of a sensorial impediment, absence of a natural impediment, and absence of a legal impediment to intercourse. The first is to avoid the case where there is a third person, then it is not seclusion, whether that third person is sighted or blind, awake or asleep, adult or a child who comprehends. Al-Mubtagha elaborated regarding the blind: if he does not observe their state, it is valid; if he is deaf, during the day it is not valid; during the night it is valid. The third includes his other wife, which is the school, based on the dislike of intercourse in the presence of a co-wife. There is difference regarding the slave-girl on multiple opinions: some say she does not prevent at all, even if she is a slave-girl belonging to someone else; some say her own slave-girl prevents, unlike his slave-girl; the chosen view is that her slave-girl does not prevent like his slave-girl, as in al-Khulasah, and fatwa is given accordingly as in al-Mubtagha. Imam al-Sarakhsi definitively stated in al-Mabsut that each of them prevents, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, because he naturally refrains from intercourse in front of his slave-girl. End. The third includes a dog if it is vicious in any case, and if not vicious, similarly if it belongs to her; if it belongs to him, the seclusion is valid. Excluded from the third is a child who does not comprehend, a madman, and one unconscious. What is meant by 'comprehending' here is one who can express what occurs between them, as in al-Khaniyyah. And to avoid a place unsuitable for seclusion: suitable is where they feel safe from being seen by others, like a house or room even without a ceiling, likewise a tent in the desert, a dome-covered area, and an orchard with a door and locked; it is not valid in a mosque, major road, bathhouse, rooftop without a screen, and an orchard without a door even if no one is there. There is difference regarding a room whose door is open or its floors such that if someone looked, he would see them... His saying: (And includes the third) i.e., occurring in his saying 'to avoid where there is a third'. His saying: (And to avoid a place unsuitable for seclusion) is conjoined to his saying 'to avoid where there is a third'. His saying: (Because his sickness does not...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of khulwah (seclusion) as one of the causes that completes the mahr, i.e., makes the full mahr obligatory. The author explains that a valid khulwah after a valid marriage contract, free from impediments, is equivalent to actual intercourse in obligating the mahr. This is based on consensus of the Companions (haka al-Tahawi ijma' al-sahabah) and a hadith from al-Daraqutni. The verse 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them' is interpreted as referring to khulwah, as touching is usually a result of seclusion. The passage then details four categories of conditions needed for khulwah to be valid: (1) actual seclusion, meaning no third person present; (2) no sensorial impediment (like illness that prevents desire); (3) no natural impediment (like extreme aversion); (4) no legal impediment (like fasting or ihram). The bulk of the passage elaborates on the 'third person' condition: various types of third persons and their effect on khulwah's validity. Key points: A third person who is a child that does not comprehend, a madman, or unconscious does not invalidate khulwah. A blind person may or may not invalidate depending on circumstances. A slave-girl's presence is debated: the preferred view (and fatwa) is that a slave-girl (whether the wife's or husband's) does not invalidate khulwah, contrary to Imam al-Sarakhsi's view that both do. A vicious dog invalidates khulwah. Regarding the place: it must provide privacy such that they feel safe from being seen; mosques, public roads, bathhouses, etc., are not suitable. The passage ends with a discussion of the suitability of a room with an open door (unclear due to truncation). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi position that khulwah is legally equivalent to intercourse for mahr purposes, with detailed conditions ensuring real potential for intimacy.

Key Terms
الخلوة (al-khulwah): The seclusion of a married couple alone in a private place, free from impediments, which in Hanafi law is equivalent to actual intercourse for obligating the full mahr.
المهر (al-mahr): The dowry or bridal gift obligatory upon the husband in a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof cited in this passage for the ruling that khulwah obligates full mahr?
A) Quranic verse on touching
B) Consensus of the Companions (ijma)
C) Analogy to divorce
D) Analogy to sale

Key Takeaway
Valid khulwah after marriage obligates full mahr in the Hanafi school, supported by consensus and hadith.

Conditions for Valid Khulwah: The Four Impediments
Conditions for valid khulwah: four categories and detailed applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of conditions for valid khulwah and apply them to determine whether a given seclusion qualifies as valid khulwah.

Core Idea
Khulwah is valid only if there is actual solitude and no sensorial, natural, or legal impediment to intercourse.

This lesson dives into the detailed conditions that make khulwah valid and equivalent to intercourse. The author categorizes them into four types: actual seclusion, and absence of sensorial, natural, and legal impediments. We will explore specific applications regarding third persons, suitable places, and other nuances.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
Identify the four conditions from the text: (1) actual seclusion, (2) no sensorial impediment, (3) no natural impediment, (4) no legal impediment.
2
Examine 'actual seclusion' in detail
Understand that actual seclusion means no third person who can observe or understand what is happening. The text specifies who counts as a third person (e.g., blind, deaf, child, slave-girl, dog) and who does not (e.g., non-comprehending child, madman).
3
Understand the place condition
The place must provide privacy such that the couple feels safe from being seen. Suitable places include houses, tents, locked orchards; unsuitable places include mosques, roads, bathhouses, rooftops without cover, open orchards.
4
Analyze the dispute on slave-girls
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the presence of a slave-girl invalidates khulwah. The text presents the views and concludes that the preferred view is that a slave-girl does not invalidate.

Key Terms
المانع الحسي (al-mani' al-hissi): A sensorial impediment to intercourse, such as illness in either spouse that physically prevents the act.
المانع الطبيعي (al-mani' al-tabi'i): A natural impediment, such as extreme aversion or repulsion that would normally prevent intercourse.
المانع الشرعي (al-mani' al-shar'i): A legal impediment, such as fasting, ihram, menstruation, or postpartum bleeding, which prohibits intercourse by sacred law.

Valid Seclusion (Khalwa) Completes the Mahr Like Intercourse
Explanation of valid seclusion as equivalent to intercourse for obligation of full mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for valid seclusion (khalwa) and apply them to determine when the full mahr becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Valid seclusion (khalwa) completes the mahr just as intercourse does, provided four impediments are absent.

In Hanafi fiqh, the full mahr (dower) becomes obligatory upon the wife not only through actual intercourse but also through valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha). This lesson unpacks the detailed conditions that make seclusion legally effective, drawing on the commentary of al-Bahr al-Raiq on Kanz al-Daqaiq. Understanding these conditions is essential for determining the wife's financial rights in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proof
The passage states that valid seclusion is like intercourse for completing the mahr, citing consensus of the Companions and the hadith of al-Darqutni. Recognize the proof structure.
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni in transmission, but the school relies on it.
2
Break down the four conditions
The author lists four conditions for seclusion to be valid: actual seclusion, no sensory impediment, no natural impediment, no legal impediment. Understand each category.
These correspond to the presence of a third person, illness/physical obstruction, menstruation/nifas, and ihram/obligatory fasting.
3
Explore sub-cases for each condition
The passage provides numerous sub-rulings: the third person (blind, deaf, child, slave girl, dog), illness (husband vs wife), physical atresia, menstruation, ihram, fasting. Trace the reasoning.
Pay special attention to the distinction between the husband's illness (generally prevents) and the wife's (only if it actually prevents intercourse).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the slave girl
The passage presents two views: the earlier view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) that the wife's slave girl prevents seclusion, and the later fatwa that it does not. Understand the tarjih mechanism.
The later fatwa is relied upon (mufta bihi) as per al-Khulasa and al-Mubtagha.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Synthesize the conditions to determine whether a specific scenario constitutes valid seclusion and thus completes the mahr.
Use the guided path as a checklist: actual seclusion? sensory impediment? natural impediment? legal impediment?

وقد حكى الطحاوي إجماع الصحابة عليه، ويدل عليه حديث الدارقطني من كشف خمار امرأة أو نظر إليها وجب الصداق، دخل أو لم يدخل وحينئذ فالمراد بالمس في قوله تعالى وإن طلقتموهن من قبل أن تمسوهن [البقرة: ٢٣٧] الخلوة إطلاقاً لاسم المسبب على السبب إذا لمس مسبب عن الخلوة عادة ويكون كما له بالجماع بحضرة الناس بالإجماع لا بالآية ومن فروع لزوم المهر بالخلوة لو زني بامرأة فتزوجها وهو على بطنها فعليه مهران مهر بالزنا لأنه سقط الحد بالتزوج قبل تمام الزنا، والمهر المسمى بالنكاح لأن هذا يزيد على الخلوة. وقد شرط المصنف في إقامتها مقام الوطء شروطاً ترجع إلى أربعة أشياء : الخلوة الحقيقية، وعدم مانع حسي، وعدم مانع طبعي، وعدم مانع شرعي من الوطء فالأول للاحتراز عما إذا كان هناك ثالث فليست بخلوة، سواء كان ذلك الثالث بصيراً أو أعمى أو يقظاناً أو نائماً بالغاً أو صبياً يعقل. وفصل في المبتغى في الأعمى فإن لم يقف على حاله تصح وإن كان أصم إن كان نهاراً لا تصح وإن كان ليلاً تصح وشمل الثالث زوجته الأخرى وهو المذهب بناء على كراهة وطئها بحضرة ضرتها. واختلف في الجارية على أقوال ؛ قيل لا تمنع مطلقاً ولو كانت جارية لغيرهما، وقيل جاريتها تمنع بخلاف جاريته والمختار أن جاريتها لا تمنع كجاريته كما في الخلاصة وعليه الفتوى كما في المبتغى، وجزم الإمام السرخسي في المبسوط بأن كلاً منهما يمنع وهو قول أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه لأنه يمتنع من غشيانها بين يدي أمته طبعاً ا هـ. وشمل الثالث الكلب إن كان عقوراً مطلقاً وإن لم يكن عقوراً فكذلك إن كان لها، وإن كان له صحت الخلوة. وخرج الثالث الصبي الذي لا يعقل والمجنون والمغمى عليه والمراد بالذي يعقل هنا ما يمكنه أن يعبر ما يكون بينهما كما في الخانية وللاحتراز عن مكان لا يصلح للخلوة، والصالح لها أن يأمنا فيه اطلاع غيرهما عليهما كالدار والبيت ولو لم يكن له سقف، وكذا الخيمة في المفازة والمحل الذي عليه قبة مضروبة وكذا البستان الذي له باب وأغلق فلا تصح في المسجد والطريق الأعظم والحمام وسطح الدار من غير ساتر والبستان الذي ليس له باب وإن لم يكن هناك أحد، واختلف في البيت إذا كان بابه مفتوحاً أو طوابقه بحيث لو نظر إنسان رآهما ففي مجموع النوازل : إن كان لا يدخل عليهما أحد إلا بإذن فهي خلوة واختار في الذخيرة أنه مانع وهو الظاهر. ويصح أن تكون هذه الفروع داخلة في المانع الحسي لأن وجود ثالث كما في الأسرار. وأشار بالمرض إلى المانع الحسي وعممه بعدم الفرق بين مرضه ومرضها . وأطلقه فأفاد أن مطلق المرض مانع وهو كذلك في مرضه، وأما في مرضها فلا بد أن يكون مرضاً يمنع الجماع أو يلحقه به ضرر وهو الصحيح لأن مرضه لا يعرى عن تكسر وفتور عادة. ومن المانع الحسي الرتق والقرن والعفل والشعر داخل الفرج المانع من جماعها، فالقرن في الفرج مانع يمنع من سلوك الذكر فيه إما غدة غليظة أو لحم أو عظم وامرأة رتقاء بها ذلك . كذا في المغرب وامرأة رتقاء بينة الرتق إذا لم يكن لها خرق إلا المبال. وضبط القرن في شرح المجمع بسكون الراء والرتق بفتح التاء، والعفل شيء مدور يخرج بالفرج ومنه صغرها بحيث لا تطيق الجماع، وليس له أن يدخل بها قبل أن تطيقه، وقدر بالبلوغ، وقيل بالتسع والأولى عدم التقدير كما قدمناه . فلو قال الزوج تطيقه وأراد الدخول وأنكر الأب فالقاضي يريها النساء ولم يعتبر السن. كذا في الخلاصة . وفي خلوة الصغير الذي لا يقدر على الجماع قولان، وجزم قاضيخان بعدم الصحة فكان هو المعتمد ولذا قيد في الذخيرة بالمراهق وسيأتي الكلام على الخصي ونحوه . وأشار بالحيض والنفاس إلى المانع الطبعي وهو شرعي أيضاً ولا يخفى أنه عند عدم درور الدم ليس مانعاً طبعاً مع أنه مانع شرعاً لأن الطهر المتخلل بين الدمين في حيض ونفاس، والظاهر أنه لا يوجد لنا مانع طبعي إلا وهو شرعي فلو اكتفوا بالمانع الشرعي عنه لكان أولى. وأشار بالإحرام والصوم إلى المانع الشرعي، أما الإحرام فأطلقه فشمل الإحرام بحج فرض أو نفل أو بعمرة وعلله في الهداية وغيرها بأنه يلزم من الوطء معه الدم وفساد النسك والقضاء، فظاهره أنه لو خلا بها بعد الوقوف بعرفة فإنها صحيحة للأمن من الفساد من أن الجواب مطلق وهو الظاهر للحرمة شرعاً. وأما الصوم فقيده المصنف بصوم الفرض للاحتراز عن صوم التطوع لأنه لا يمنع صحة الخلوة وإن كان واجباً بالشروع لأن وجوبه لضرورة صيانة المؤدى فلا يظهر في حق غيره مع أن الإفطار فيه بغير عذر جائز في رواية .
His saying (And seclusion without illness of one of them, menstruation, postnatal bleeding, ihram, and obligatory fasting is like intercourse) is an explanation of the third cause that completes the mahr, which is valid seclusion, because it delivers the exchange (the woman) by removing impediments, and that is within her capacity, so her right to the exchange is confirmed by analogy with sale. Al-Tahawi reported the consensus of the Companions upon it, and it is supported by the hadith of al-Darqutni: 'Whoever uncovers a woman's veil or looks at her, the dowry becomes obligatory, whether he entered or not.' Therefore the meaning of 'touch' in His statement 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them' [2:237] is seclusion, as a name for the effect is applied to the cause, since touching is normally the result of seclusion. And it is like intercourse in the presence of people by consensus, not by the verse. Among the subsidiary issues of the obligation of mahr by seclusion: if a man commits zina with a woman then marries her while he is on top of her, two mahr are due: mahr for zina because the hadd penalty is dropped by the marriage before completion of zina, and the named mahr in the marriage because this exceeds seclusion. And the author stipulated for it to stand in place of intercourse conditions that revert to four things: actual seclusion, absence of a sensory impediment, absence of a natural impediment, and absence of a legal impediment to intercourse. The first is to avoid the case where there is a third person, for then it is not seclusion, whether that third person is sighted or blind, awake or asleep, adult or a discerning child. And he detailed in al-Mubtagha regarding the blind: if he does not know their state, it is valid; if he is deaf, if during the day it is not valid, if at night it is valid. And the third person includes his other wife, which is the madhhab based on the dislike of having intercourse with her in the presence of her co-wife. And there is a difference of opinion regarding the slave girl: some say she does not prevent absolutely even if she is the slave of someone else; some say his wife's slave girl prevents but his own slave girl does not; the chosen view is that her slave girl does not prevent like his slave girl, as in al-Khulasa, and the fatwa is according to that as in al-Mubtagha. Imam al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut stated that both prevent, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa and his two companions, because one naturally refrains from having intercourse with her in front of his slave girl. He said: And the third person includes the dog if it is ferocious absolutely, and if it is not ferocious then similarly if it belongs to her; if it belongs to him, the seclusion is valid. And excluded from the third person are the non-discerning child, the insane, and the unconscious. The meaning of 'discerning' here is one who can describe what happens between them, as in al-Khaniyya. And to avoid a place unsuitable for seclusion. Suitable for it is where they can feel safe from others seeing them, like a house or room even if it has no roof, likewise a tent in the desert, a place with a dome erected, likewise an orchard with a door that is closed. It is not valid in a mosque, a major road, a bathhouse, a roof without a screen, and an orchard without a door even if no one is there. There is a difference of opinion regarding a house whose door is open or its shutters so that if someone looked he would see them: in Majmu' al-Nawazil: if no one enters upon them except with permission, it is seclusion; and in al-Dhakhira he chose that it prevents, and that is apparent. And it is possible that these branches are included under sensory impediment because the presence of a third person is sensory, as in al-Asrar. And he indicated by 'illness' the sensory impediment, and he made it general by not differentiating between his illness and hers. And he made it absolute, indicating that absolute illness prevents, and that is so regarding his illness; as for her illness, it must be an illness that prevents intercourse or causes harm thereby, and that is the correct view, because his illness is usually not free from lethargy and weakness. Among the sensory impediments are: atresia, adhesion, cyst, and hair inside the vagina preventing intercourse. 'Adhesion' (qarn) in the vagina prevents entry of the male organ, either a thick gland, flesh, or bone, and a woman with that condition is 'ratqa'. As in al-Mughrib: a woman with obvious atresia (ratq) is one who has no opening except for urine. And 'qarn' is pronounced with sukun on the ra, and 'ratq' with fatha on the ta. And 'afl' is something round that protrudes from the vagina, including its being too small to tolerate intercourse. He may not enter her before she can tolerate it, and it is estimated by puberty, some say by nine years, but the better view is not to set an estimate as we have mentioned. So if the husband says she can tolerate it and wants to enter, and the father denies it, the judge shows her to women and does not consider age. As in al-Khulasa. And regarding seclusion of a young boy who cannot have intercourse, there are two opinions; Qadikhan decided on unsoundness, so it is relied upon, hence in al-Dhakhira it is restricted to the adolescent. And the discussion on the eunuch and similar will come later. And he indicated by menstruation and postnatal bleeding the natural impediment, which is also legal. It is not hidden that when there is no flow of blood it is not a natural impediment while it is a legal impediment, because the pure period between two bleedings in menstruation and postnatal bleeding is pure, and it appears that we do not have a natural impediment except that it is also legal, so if they sufficed with the legal impediment it would be better. And he indicated by ihram and fasting the legal impediment. As for ihram, he made it absolute, so it includes ihram for obligatory or supererogatory hajj or umrah. He explained in al-Hidayah and others that intercourse with it entails blood (dam), invalidation of the ritual, and making it up. Apparently, if he secluded with her after standing at Arafat, it is valid because of safety from invalidation, and the answer is absolute, and that is apparent due to the prohibition legally. As for fasting, the author restricted it to obligatory fasting to avoid voluntary fasting, because it does not prevent the validity of seclusion even if it becomes obligatory by commencing, because its obligation is to safeguard the performance of the act, so it does not appear with respect to another, and breaking it without excuse is permissible in one narration.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq explains that valid seclusion (khalwa) between spouses completes the mahr just as actual intercourse does. The author begins by citing the consensus of the Companions and the hadith of al-Darqutni as proof. He then clarifies that the Quranic term 'mess' (touch) refers to seclusion through synecdoche. The core of the passage lists four conditions for seclusion to be valid: actual seclusion (no third person), no sensory impediment (like illness or physical obstruction), no natural impediment (like menstruation), and no legal impediment (like ihram or obligatory fasting). The commentary then exhaustively details each condition. For the third person, it discusses the blind, the deaf, the wife's other wife, the slave girl, the dog, the non-discerning child, etc., and presents the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the slave girl: the relied-upon fatwa (from al-Khulasa and al-Mubtagha) is that the wife's slave girl does not prevent seclusion, contrary to the earlier view of Abu Hanifa and his two companions as reported by al-Sarakhsi. For the sensory impediment of illness, the text distinguishes between the husband's illness (which generally prevents due to lethargy) and the wife's illness (which must actually prevent intercourse). Physical obstructions like ratq, qarn, and 'afl are discussed as sensory impediments. For the natural impediment, menstruation and postnatal bleeding are legal impediments even when not flowing. For legal impediments, ihram (any type) prevents seclusion because intercourse would invalidate the ritual, but seclusion after standing at Arafat is valid as there is no risk of invalidation. Only obligatory fasting prevents seclusion, not voluntary or supererogatory fasting. The lesson provides a thorough fiqh education on the conditions of valid seclusion and their effects on mahr.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwa): Valid seclusion between spouses in a place where they are safe from observation, which completes the mahr.
مهر (mahr): The dower obligatory upon the husband by marriage, which becomes fully due upon valid seclusion or intercourse.
مانع حسي (mani' hissi): A sensual or physical impediment that prevents valid seclusion, such as illness, partial atresia, or a physical barrier.
مانع شرعي (mani' shar'i): A legal impediment that prevents valid seclusion, such as ihram (state of consecration) or obligatory fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling if a man has valid seclusion with his wife while he is in the state of ihram (for hajj)?
A) The seclusion is invalid and the mahr is not completed.
B) The seclusion is valid and the mahr is completed.
C) The seclusion is valid, but the mahr is not completed because ihram prevents the completion of mahr.
D) The seclusion is invalid only if the ihram is for obligatory hajj; if voluntary, it is valid.

Key Takeaway
Valid seclusion in Hanafi law completes the mahr if four categories of impediments are absent, reflecting the importance of privacy in marital rights.

Physical Preventers of Valid Khalwa in Hanafi Marriage Law
Expansion of the ruling on physical preventers of valid seclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the physical conditions that invalidate khalwa and state the Hanafi ruling on full mahr when seclusion is prevented.

Core Idea
Khalwa requires no physical obstacle to intercourse; illness or anatomical defects nullify it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the physical preventers (manea' hissi) that invalidate seclusion and thus prevent the obligation of full mahr. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between the husband's illness (always a preventer) and the wife's illness (only if it prevents intercourse or causes harm). Additionally, specific anatomical conditions such as ratq, qarn, and 'afl are listed as physical preventers. Understanding these conditions is essential for determining the legal consequences of seclusion after a valid marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Define khalwa and its legal effect
Khalwa is seclusion without a third person entering without permission. If valid, it makes full mahr wajib.
2
Identify the physical preventers listed
The text lists illness (with asymmetry between spouses), and anatomical conditions: ratq, qarn, 'afl, and hair inside the vagina.
3
Distinguish the husband's vs. wife's illness
Husband's illness is always a preventer due to usual weakness; wife's illness only if it prevents intercourse or causes harm.
This distinction is based on the differing impact of illness on each spouse.
4
Examine the anatomical preventers
Define each: ratq (no opening except for urination), qarn (growth inside), 'afl (protrusion), and hair. These prevent intercourse.
These conditions are physical and objectively determinable.
5
Apply the ruling to minors
For a minor female, intercourse is not possible until she can bear it, estimated by puberty or age nine; seclusion with a minor male incapable of intercourse is invalid per Qadikhan.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب المهر
مجموع النوازل : إن كان لا يدخل عليهما أحد إلا بإذن فهي خلوة واختار في الذخيرة أنه مانع وهو الظاهر. ويصح أن تكون هذه الفروع داخلة في المانع الحسي لأن وجود ثالث كما في الأسرار. وأشار بالمرض إلى المانع الحسي وعممه وعدم صلاحية المكان مانع حسي بعدم الفرق بين مرضه ومرضها . وأطلقه فأفاد أن مطلق المرض مانع وهو كذلك في مرضه، وأما في مرضها فلا بد أن يكون مرضاً يمنع الجماع أو يلحقه به ضرر وهو الصحيح لأن مرضه لا يعرى عن تكسر وفتور عادة. ومن المانع الحسي الرتق والقرن والعفل والشعر داخل الفرج المانع من جماعها، فالقرن في الفرج مانع يمنع من سلوك الذكر فيه إما غدة غليظة أو لحم أو عظم وامرأة رتقاء بها ذلك . كذا في المغرب وامرأة رتقاء بينة الرتق إذا لم يكن لها خرق إلا المبال. وضبط القرن في شرح المجمع بسكون الراء والرتق بفتح التاء، والعفل شيء مدور يخرج بالفرج ومنه صغرها بحيث لا تطيق الجماع، وليس له أن يدخل بها قبل أن تطيقه، وقدر بالبلوغ، وقيل بالتسع والأولى عدم التقدير كما قدمناه . فلو قال الزوج تطيقه وأراد الدخول وأنكر الأب فالقاضي يريها النساء ولم يعتبر السن. كذا في الخلاصة . وفي خلوة الصغير الذي لا يقدر على الجماع قولان، وجزم قاضيخان بعدم الصحة فكان هو المعتمد ولذا قيد في الذخيرة بالمراهق وسيأتي الكلام على الخصي ونحوه .
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Mahr. Majmu' al-Nawazil: If no one enters upon them except with permission, it is khalwa (seclusion). And he chose in al-Dhakhira that it is a preventer, and that is apparent. And it is valid that these branches are included in the physical preventer because the presence of a third person is as in al-Asrar. And he indicated by illness the physical preventer, and generalized it, and the unsuitability of the place is a physical preventer due to the lack of difference between his illness and her illness. And he gave it absolutely, so it indicates that absolute illness is a preventer, and that is so in his illness. As for her illness, it must be an illness that prevents intercourse or causes harm to him thereby, and that is the correct (view) because his illness is not free from weakness and languor usually. And among the physical preventers are ratq, qarn, 'afl, and hair inside the vagina that prevents intercourse. Qarn in the vagina is a preventer that prevents the entry of the male organ, either a thick gland, flesh, or bone; and a woman with ratq (is one) who has that. Thus in al-Mughrib. And a woman with clear ratq is one who has no opening except for urination. And the definition of qarn in Sharh al-Majma' is with sukun on the ra', and ratq with fatha on the ta', and 'afl is a round thing that comes out of the vagina, and among it is her smallness such that she cannot bear intercourse. And he does not have the right to enter upon her before she can bear it, and it is estimated by puberty, and some said by nine years, and the preferable is not to estimate as we have mentioned. So if the husband says she can bear it and wants to enter and the father denies, the judge has women inspect her and does not consider age. Thus in al-Khulasa. And regarding the seclusion of a minor who cannot have intercourse, there are two opinions, and Qadikhan affirmed its invalidity, so that is the relied-upon (view). Hence he restricted in al-Dhakhira to the adolescent, and discussion of the eunuch and similar will come.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on valid seclusion (khalwa) that makes the full mahr obligatory. The commentator discusses physical preventers (manea' hissi). First, the presence of a third person is a preventer. Illness is a general preventer: in the husband, any illness suffices due to usual weakness; in the wife, only an illness that prevents intercourse or causes harm. Other physical preventers: ratq (closed vagina), qarn (growth inside), 'afl (protrusion), and hair inside the vagina. These prevent intercourse. For a minor female, intercourse is not possible until she can bear it, estimated by puberty or age nine; the preferred view is no fixed age. If dispute arises, women inspect her. Seclusion with a minor male incapable of intercourse is invalid per Qadikhan (mu'tamad). The text follows the Hanafi school's careful consideration of actual physical capability.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwa): Seclusion between spouses without a third person entering without permission, which makes full mahr obligatory.
رتق (ratq): A condition where the woman's vagina has no opening except for urination, preventing intercourse.
قرن (qarn): A growth (gland, flesh, or bone) inside the vagina that prevents entry.
عفل ('afl): A round protrusion emerging from the vagina that prevents intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a physical preventer that invalidates khalwa?
A) Wife's ratq
B) Husband's mild cold
C) Presence of a child who can speak
D) Wife's dislike of her husband

Key Takeaway
Physical preventers such as illness and anatomical abnormalities nullify khalwa, preventing the obligation of full mahr; the husband's illness is always a preventer, but the wife's only if it prevents intercourse or causes harm.

Natural and Shar'i Preventers of Valid Khalwa
Commentary on preventers of khalwa with focus on fasting dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural and shar'i preventers of khalwa, identify which types of fasting and prayer invalidate seclusion according to the correct Hanafi view, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding make-up and vowed fasts.

Core Idea
Only the current Ramadan fast and current obligatory prayer prevent valid khalwa; other fasts and prayers do not, because their breach lacks kaffara.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the preventers (mawani') that invalidate a valid seclusion (khalwa) in marriage, thereby making the full mahr obligatory. The author distinguishes between natural preventers like menstruation and shar'i preventers like ihram and fasting. The main focus is on which types of fasting and prayer constitute a shar'i preventer, with a detailed intra-madhhab dispute about whether only the current Ramadan fast prevents or also other obligatory fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of preventers
Begin by noting that the passage classifies preventers into natural (tabi'i) and shar'i (legal). Natural preventers include menstruation (hayd) and postnatal bleeding (nifas). Shar'i preventers include ihram, fasting, and prayer. Understand that natural preventers are also considered shar'i because they prohibit intercourse legally.
The author notes that there is no natural preventer that is not also shar'i.
2
Analyze the rule for ihram as a preventer
Examine the statement that ihram – whether for obligatory or supererogatory hajj or 'umra – prevents valid khalwa because intercourse during ihram incurs blood (dam), corruption of the ritual (fasad al-nusuk), and make-up (qada'). Note that after standing at 'Arafa or completing most of the umra tawaf, the danger of fasad is removed, so seclusion may be valid.
The author emphasizes that ihram is absolute in its prevention due to the serious consequences.
3
Work through the fasting dispute
The matn restricts the preventer to 'obligatory fast' (sawm al-fard). Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute: some hold that any obligatory fast (make-up, expiation, vowed) prevents khalwa, while the correct view (asahh) is that only the current Ramadan fast (ada') prevents. The key evidence is that breaking the current Ramadan fast requires kaffara and qada', whereas other obligatory fasts lack kaffara, making their prohibition weaker.
The text clarifies that supererogatory fasts never prevent because they can be broken without sin.
4
Apply the same principle to prayer
The passage extends the ruling to prayer: obligatory prayer (especially the current one) prevents khalwa, while supererogatory prayer does not. Note that for prayer, even make-up (qada') may not prevent according to the correct view, as the prohibition in its breaking is the same as for current prayer, but the author leans toward only current prayer preventing.
This is consistent with the principle that only acts whose breach incurs a heavy penalty (e.g., kaffara) are strong enough preventers.

And this is supported by their statement 'its obligatory is like the obligatory fast and its supererogatory is like its supererogatory', but the reasoning for fasting is not apparent in prayer because the prohibition in breaking its current and make-up is the same. Also, what the author mentioned from Ghayat al-Bayan is apparent in the lack of difference between its current and make-up, unless one claims the difference that the prohibition in breaking the current is stronger due to probability.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on preventers (manea') of valid khalwa. It covers natural preventers (hayd, nifas) which are both natural and shar'i, and then focuses on shar'i preventers: ihram and fasting. For ihram, any form (hajj or 'umra) prevents khalwa because intercourse would require blood, fasad, and qada'. However, if seclusion occurs after the main ritual (e.g., after wuquf at 'Arafa), the ruling may differ as the danger of fasad is removed. For fasting, the matn restricts to obligatory fast (fard). The commentator discusses whether only the current Ramadan fast prevents, or also make-up, expiation, and vowed fasts. Some hold that any obligatory fast prevents; the correct view is that only current Ramadan fast prevents because breaking it incurs kaffara. Supererogatory fast does not prevent because one can break it without sin. For prayer, the same principle applies: obligatory prayer (especially current) prevents, supererogatory does not. The passage includes a detailed debate about the correct view and the author's choice, with references to Qadikhan, al-Nahr, and others. The relied-upon position is that only the current Ramadan fast (and current obligatory prayer) prevents; other obligatory fasts and prayers have two narrations, but the more correct view (as per the text) is that they do not prevent.

Key Terms
مانع طباعي (manea' tabi'i): A natural preventer such as menstruation or postnatal bleeding.
مانع شرعي (manea' shar'i): A legal preventer such as ihram, fasting, or prayer that prohibits intercourse and thus invalidates khalwa.
صوم الفرض (sawm al-fard): Obligatory fasting, specifically the current Ramadan fast (ada') according to the correct view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fast of Ramadan (current month)
B) A make-up fast for missed Ramadan days
C) A vowed (nadhr) fast
D) A supererogatory fast on Monday

Key Takeaway
Only the current Ramadan fast (ada') and current obligatory prayer are strong enough preventers to invalidate khalwa; other fasts and prayers do not affect its validity.

Fasting and Prayer as Legal Preventers of Khalwa? The Hanafi Position
Expands on rulings about fasting and prayer preventing valid khalwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that neither obligatory nor supererogatory fasting prevents valid khalwa, explain the istihsan-based proof, and apply the same reasoning to prayer.

Core Idea
No kaffarah for spoiling fast means it is not a legal preventer; prayer likewise does not prevent, per the relied-upon view.

When a marriage contract is valid, seclusion (khalwa) triggers certain legal consequences as if intercourse occurred. But are there states that prevent this effect? This lesson examines whether fasting and prayer are legal preventers (mawani') that invalidate khalwa. The correct Hanafi position, supported by istihsan, is that they are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
Khalwa is seclusion after marriage that triggers full mahr and other rulings. The question: does engaging in fasting or prayer while in seclusion prevent these consequences? The passage discusses whether fasting (obligatory and supererogatory) and prayer are legal preventers.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on fasting
Some Hanafi scholars hold that obligatory fasting prevents khalwa validity. The correct view (al-sahih) is that it does not, because spoiling a fast carries no kaffarah (expiation). The matn author is critiqued for specifying 'obligatory fasting' when the correct view applies to all fasts.
Fasting is analogous to ihram, which does prevent. But istihsan rejects qiyas due to the absence of kaffarah.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is istihsan based on the principle that kaffarah is a marker of a strong prohibition. Since there is no kaffarah for breaking a fast (unlike for ihram), fasting is not a legal preventer. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the sin of spoiling alone is insufficient.
Contrast: ihram has kaffarah, so it prevents.
4
Extend the ruling to prayer by analogy
The analogy: obligatory prayer is like obligatory fasting, supererogatory prayer like supererogatory fasting. Since there is no kaffarah for spoiling prayer, does it also fail to prevent? The relied-upon view is that it does not, although the commentator notes a flaw: spoiling any prayer is sinful.
Some argued that confirmed sunnahs (like 4 rakat before Zuhr) might prevent, but the mainstream view rejects this.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi and note the critical perspective
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that neither fasting nor prayer prevents the validity of khalwa. The commentator's critique shows that the reasoning from 'no kaffarah' is applied, but he points out the inconsistency regarding the sin of spoiling. The school's final position relies on the absence of kaffarah as the decisive criterion.
This lesson's ruling is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل صوم الفرض قضاء رمضان والكفارات والمنذور فإنها تمنع صحة الخلوة وهو قول البعض، والصحيح أنه لا يمنع صحتها لأنها لا كفارة في إفسادها، فلو قال المصنف «وصوم رمضان أي أداء كما في المجمع لكان أولى لأنه الصحيح، أو قال «والصوم اختيار القول البعض لأمكن لأنه لا فرق عند البعض بين صوم التطوع والفرض في أنه يمنع صحتها كالإحرام، فتقييده بصوم الفرض ليس على قول من الأقوال وينبغي أن يكون صوم الفرض ولو منذوراً يمنع صحة الخلوة اتفاقاً لأنه يحرم إفساده وإن كان لا كفارة فيه فهو مانع شرعي. وأما الصلاة فقالوا : فرضها كفرض الصوم ونفلها كنفله كذا في الهداية. وعلله في غاية البيان بأنه لا يأثم بترك النافلة وهو الصحيح فلا يكون مانعاً بخلاف صلاة الفرض فإنه يأثم بتركها ا . وفيه نظر لأنه ليس الكلام في الترك وإنما هو في الإفساد، ولا شك أن إفساد الصلاة لغير عذر حرام فرضاً كانت أو نفلاً، فينبغي أن يكون مطلق الصلاة مانعاً مع أنهم قالوا : إن الصلاة الواجبة كالنفل لا تمنع صحة الخلوة كما في شرح النقاية مع أنه يأثم بتركها، وأغرب منه ما في المحيط أن صلاة التطوع لا تمنع صحتها إلا الأربع قبل الظهر فإنها تمنع صحة الخلوة لأنها سنة مؤكدة فلا يجوز تركها بمثل هذا العذر ا هـ فإنه يقتضي عدم الفرق بين السنن المؤكدة ويقتضي أن الواجبة تمنع صحتها بالأولى.
And it includes obligatory fasting, the make-up of Ramadan, expiations, and vowed fasts. They indeed prevent the validity of seclusion, and that is the saying of some. The correct view is that it does not prevent its validity because there is no expiation for spoiling it. If the author had said 'and the fast of Ramadan, i.e., performing it as in al-Majma', it would have been more appropriate because it is the correct view. Or if he said 'and fasting' choosing the saying of some, it would have been possible because there is no difference according to some between supererogatory and obligatory fasting in that it prevents its validity like ihram. So his restriction to obligatory fasting is not in accordance with any of the opinions. But there is a consideration because the discussion is not about leaving but about spoiling. There is no doubt that spoiling prayer without an excuse is unlawful whether obligatory or supererogatory, so it should be that all prayer is a preventer, even though they said that obligatory prayer is like supererogatory in not preventing the validity of seclusion, as in Sharh al-Niqayah, although one sins by leaving it. And more strange is what is in al-Muhit that supererogatory prayer does not prevent its validity except the four before Zuhr, for they prevent the validity of seclusion because they are a confirmed sunnah, so it is not permissible to leave them for such an excuse. This implies no difference between confirmed sunnahs, and implies that obligatory prayer prevents its validity a fortiori.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (expansion) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the chapter of mahr. The author discusses what constitutes a legal preventer (مانع شرعي) that bars the validity of khalwa (seclusion) after a valid marriage contract. The base text states that fasting does not prevent khalwa validity, but the commentator notes that some scholars (البعض) hold that obligatory fasting (including Ramadan, make-up, expiation, and vowed fasts) does prevent it. The correct (الصحيح) position in the Hanafi school, as stated by the commentator, is that fasting does NOT prevent validity because there is no kaffarah (expiation) for spoiling it, unlike ihram which has expiation. The commentator suggests that the matn author should have specified 'Ramadan performance' rather than 'obligatory fasting' to align with the correct view. He then addresses prayer analogously: obligatory prayer (salah) is like obligatory fasting, and supererogatory prayer is like supererogatory fasting. The proof is that one does not sin by leaving supererogatory prayer, so it cannot be a legal preventer. However, the commentator notes a flaw: the discussion is about spoiling, not leaving. Spoiling any prayer without excuse is sinful, so logically all prayer should prevent validity. He then cites a strange ruling from al-Muhit that only the four rak'ahs before Zuhr (a confirmed sunnah) prevent validity, implying confirmed sunnahs are stronger. The passage ends with the implication that obligatory prayer would prevent validity a fortiori, but the school's relied-upon ruling (mufta bihi) is that it does not. The commentator is critically engaging with the matn and earlier commentaries.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwa): Seclusion between a husband and wife after a valid marriage contract in a place where intercourse is possible, which legally triggers certain consequences (e.g., full mahr, iddah).
مانع شرعي (mani' shar'i): A legal preventative that bars khalwa from having its usual legal effect (e.g., fasting, ihram, menses).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi position, does an obligatory fast (e.g., Ramadan) prevent the validity of khalwa (seclusion) after marriage?
A) Yes, because it is sinful to spoil it.
B) No, because there is no kaffarah (expiation) for spoiling it.
C) Yes, but only for Ramadan, not for other obligatory fasts.
D) No, because seclusion is always valid regardless of fasting.

Key Takeaway
Fasting and prayer, even if obligatory, do not prevent valid khalwa because the decisive criterion is the presence of kaffarah, which they lack.
